## 1. Part p01 - Introduction to Mathematical Logic

Welcome everybody. This is the introductory video for both MATH 125A and MATH 135. So these two courses are both logic courses but they are independent of each other. They are different topics and they don't need each other. They're both mathematical logic and one of them, as we're going to see, is about the foundations of logic and formal systems. And the other one is more about the axioms to develop other mathematics. I'll get into that in a second.

So, but they have the very basic ideas, it's common, so that's why I'm doing this joint video. So one of the main goals of mathematical logic is to develop the foundations for mathematics. There are other goals, but we do other things in mathematical logic. But one of them is that, to develop the foundations for all of mathematics. So if you imagine mathematics as a big building, that is where things are built on top of each other, like a big edifice, then logic, one of the aspects of logic is to look at the foundations of that. What is all this standing on?

So the first thing we need to look at is formal systems. This would be a place where we can develop mathematics in a formal way. What are these formal systems good for? So what do we need them for? So what I mentioned before that they are going to give us the foundations for the whole edifice of mathematics. So in a way, they tell us that we can be, if you're doing something in the formal system, we can be sure that it's going to be correct.

And there is no subjectivity to it. There is no, I'm trying to convince this other mathematician about it. It's like, this is a formal system. But that's in theory, because in practice, it's very hard to actually do a proof in the formal system. But it's good to know the foundations are there. I'll get back to this thing about doing it in a second. For instance, a hundred and thirty, forty years ago, Cantor and Bertrand Russell and other people started to find paradoxes, reasonings that were completely SOUND, that looked like the reasonings mathematicians were doing at the time.

And that led to contradictions, like for instance, the barber paradox, which in set theory becomes considered a set of all the sets that don't contain themselves. I don't know if you heard about this, but once mathematics was becoming more abstract and abstract and people were doing abstract proofs, then it was easy to fall into the trap of doing something that is not allowed or that will lead to a contradiction.

So it was good to develop something where we can know that we are standing on a strong footing. **A second reason why formal systems are GOOD is that in logic we can study them as mathematical objects themselves, because now a formal system, a proof, a theorem, a construction, they become themselves concrete mathematical objects.** So we can study proofs, theorems, in a mathematical way. So we can use mathematics to study mathematics itself.

So that's why it's called metamathematics. **And that's how we can prove Gödel's theorem, that some things are NOT provable.** For that we need to be able to prove about things being provable or not. And a third application is that computers can play with formal systems. That's a more modern thing, but it's actually becoming more and more popular. So I was saying that in practice, it's very hard to write a proof in a formal system.

Like when you write a proof, you're writing in English and you're just trying to convince a mathematical reader that your argument has no holes and it's like perfectly logical and SOUND. But if you want to write your full proof like step by step in a formal system, you go crazy. But if you manage to do it and you do it on a computer, a computer can go and check step by step that every step you did was correct.

You don't need a human to do that because now it's very formal. So now you have essentially a proof that your proof is 100% correct. And that's actually useful for some things. So it started to be useful for some programs, like to verify that a program is doing what it's supposed to be doing. So to prove that a program is doing what it's supposed to do, which is sometimes very important if the program is controlling something that is very important, like, I don't know, your heart.

Now, in the last couple decades, people started to actually prove, like write down full detail proofs in a formal system of some well-known theorems in mathematics. And sometimes these tasks are like huge because like for big theorems, there are a lot of little things that are not written. But if you want to convince the computer that everything is right, you need to add every step. And now there are programs that help you do the silly, the small steps.

And so you don't go too crazy. You still go a bit crazy, I guess. So right, having a formal system allow us to now get computers to help us verify proofs and maybe even write some. To start into these formal systems, one thing we need to think about is, okay, what is a proof? Here is a definition. A proof is an argument that uses logical steps to show that a mathematical statement follows from certain assumptions.

Let's get deeper into what this actually means. There are a few things here that are important. First, what we're talking about proving here are concrete mathematical statements. We're not talking about proving an ambiguous statement about the weather. We're talking about concrete mathematical statements. So we need to be explicit about what we mean by a concrete mathematical statement. And for that, we need to define a formal language.

A proof is made out of logical steps. We know in practice when writing a proof, we can use any reasoning we like so long as everybody agrees that the steps we are taking are logical. But if we want to talk about proofs as concrete objects, to be able to prove things about them, we need to be explicit about which logical steps we are allowed to use. These logical steps are called rules.

Third, we need assumptions. When you write a proof, you always use previous knowledge. That previous knowledge usually comes in forms of theorems that were proved before. And those theorems use previous knowledge. And if you keep on going backwards, you will eventually reach statements that are so basic that you just cannot prove. But they are basic enough that you don't need to prove them. Those are the axioms.

More than 100 years ago, this man wanted to build a formal system for all those mathematics where all statements could be proved in a purely formal and Syntactic way. By that I mean in a way that only involves manipulating symbols following certain rules in a purely mechanical way without having to even know what the symbols mean. This way, you could be sure about something being true or not in a purely mechanical way that nobody could argue with.

Let's see how that worked out at the end. So let's see what a formal system is. It consists of three things. A language, a set of rules, and a list of axioms. The language. To define a language, we need symbols. They are like the letters of the alphabet. And grammatical rules that tell us how to put these symbols together. Here is a standard list of symbols. **The first symbols, 0, 1, plus, times, belongs to, form what is called a vocabulary.**

And those are variables. You can change them for some other symbols if you want to work with something else. These ones are good enough. The latter ones, equality and or not exist for all. **The variable symbols and the parentheses are called the logical symbols.** And they are essentially fixed in all first-order logic. You can modify them slightly. For instance, here, you can add the implication symbol because you can just define it from the other.

So you may add it or not. But essentially, these are the logical symbols. Okay.

Then we need to put these symbols together. And for that, we need grammatical rules. Here's a standard set of rules, but don't worry about the details. I don't want to get into them right now. I just want you to see what they look like. Essentially, when you see a string of symbols, you're going to be able to tell if it makes sense or doesn't. For instance, this one here obviously doesn't make sense.

While this other one, for every x there exists a y such as y plus y equals x or y plus y equals x plus 1. It's a sequence of symbols that makes sense. Alright, let's go into the rules. These are the rules of logical thinking. The rules that we use when we write proofs. For example, there is a rule that says that if you can prove not phi, that is, the negation of phi where phi is a grammatically correct sequence of symbols.

And you can prove phi or psi, then you can prove psi. For instance, if phi was the sentence x equals y and psi was the sentence z equals 1, then this rule will read as follows. We express such rules in the following format. if you can prove the statements on the top, you can prove the statements on the bottom. Here's an example of a full set of rules of first-order logic.

Again, don't worry about the details. I just wanted to show you what they look like. Axioms. These are the statements that describe the very basic behavior of whatever you're working with. Numbers, sets, groups, rings, whatever you're working with. The axioms don't need to be proved. They are used as basic assumptions within our proofs. if you're going to use them in our proof, they better be true. So, they better be obviously true.

And hopefully, we'll have enough axioms to derive everything we want. As we'll see, this is too much to hope for. Here's an example of a list of axioms. These are the Peano axioms. They are the standard axioms to work with when you're working with the natural numbers. They start with the very basic properties of 0, 1, plus, and times. And then we have the axioms for induction, which allow you to do proofs by induction.

There is another list of axioms that is used to axiomatize all of mathematics. It's called the Zermelo-Fraenkel set theory. Okay, so now we know what a formal system is. Here come the key points. The language is complete. So, all mathematical statements can be expressed in this language. Once you get used to working in this language, you're going to see that every mathematical statement you want to make, you can make it in this formal language using these grammatical rules.

So, that's good. Alright, then the rules. They are also complete. Okay, this is not a simple observation like the previous ones. There is no reason to believe at first that these few rules are going to be enough to formalize all arguments mathematicians want to make. Arguments come in all shapes and forms. But surprisingly, they are enough. This is another of Gödel's famous terms. It's called the COMPLETENESS theorem.

And it says that if you can prove something, you can prove it using only these rules. It actually says that if a statement is true in all possible universes, then it can be proved using these rules. Okay, there are some subtleties here that I'm leaving for another time. The axioms, though, are not complete. **And this is what the INCOMPLETENESS theorem says.** Gödel proved both the COMPLETENESS and INCOMPLETENESS theorems.

It's not that he couldn't make up his mind about COMPLETENESS or INCOMPLETENESS. It's just that COMPLETENESS was about the rules and INCOMPLETENESS was about the axioms. Okay, so the two courses split right here. So 125 is going to be about the language part and the rules part of the formal system. **And also about semantics, about the language and provability and other things.** We're going to prove those COMPLETENESS theorems that we just mentioned.

And the Gödel one is not an easy one. **Gödel COMPLETENESS theorem, that's going to be our main theorem at the end of the class.** And then 135 is going to be about the axioms part. And in that one, we're going to develop the axioms for set theory. And set theory, these are the axioms where all mathematics can be done. So these are axioms for all of mathematics and they are kind of now widely accepted as the axioms for mathematics.

Which, as I mentioned, they are not complete. I mean, the area of set theory of logic studies theorems of mathematics or statements that cannot be proved from the axioms that are beyond these axioms. Well, we're not going to get into that. Maybe we're going to mention a couple of those. But we're not going to get into those much. We're just going to go slowly through the axioms to understand the whole development of set theory and how it works as a foundation for mathematics.

And we're going to look at something that follows from this development, which is the study of ordinals and cardinals that are very important in set theory. Okay, that's it for today. Next time we'll do separate videos, one for 125A, one for MATH 135, and we'll go into the different topics. See you next time.

## 3. Part p03 - The Language of Sentential Logic

Welcome everybody. Okay, so this class is going to be on sentential logic. In this course, it's going to be mostly about something we call first-order logic, where we can develop all of mathematics. **But to start with, we're going to start with a simpler logic, which is called sentential logic.** And it's not very good to do all of mathematics, but it's going to be a good toy example to see how logic works and to learn some of the main ideas that we need to learn.

But it's still not completely useless. I mean, it's used quite a bit in computer science for circuits. It's used in computer science to just figure some things in machine learning. So it's still quite useful. And we're going to learn quite a bit about it. And it's going to be a good stepping stone for them when we do the logic. We're going to have some ideas that we need.

If you didn't watch the video on strings, you should watch it before here. Okay, so in sentential logic, it's called sentential logic because the basic building blocks for the logic are sentences, which can be either true or false. So those are the building blocks. They're not numbers or anything. Building blocks are sentences, and then you build from them. One example, it can be like the following sentence.

If Dumbo can fly, Dumbo is pink. Okay, that's a weird sentence. It's a sentence. Second sentence is that Dumbo can fly. So from these two sentences, we can deduce that Dumbo is pink. It doesn't matter if these sentences make sense or not. What we care about here is the logic that we're using to derive on whether the logic is SOUND. In this case, the logic is SOUND.

We're going to be looking at more interesting reasonings later. But essentially, we're going to ignore the content of each particular sentence, these building blocks. And we're going to look at the logic that's behind it. So this particular reasoning, we're going to write as follows. We're going to write as P implies Q. if you know this, and if you know P, then from those two things, we can deduce Q.

Right? From P implies Q, and from P we deduce Q. Where here, Dumbo is pink is Q. So that's the sentence. So Q is a building block, and it's Dumbo is pink. P is another building block, and it's Dumbo can fly. That's P. And we're only going to concentrate on that particular form for the reasoning. Okay, so the thing that we need to look at today is the language for sentational logic.

So we're going to develop a formal language where we can deal with this logic. Okay, so to develop a language, we need a couple of things. One is a set of symbols, and the other is the grammar that is going to allow us to put the symbols together in a way that makes sense. Alright? So what are going to be the symbols for sentational logic? So we're going to have a bunch of symbols, not too many, actually, but infinitely many, I guess.

So most of them are similar. So we have parentheses, and then we have, these are kind of punctuation symbols. **And THEN we have what are called the connectors, which are these guys up here, NOT, and, or, implies, IF AND ONLY IF.** And then we have variable symbols that are going to represent sentences, like the P and the Q. In the example before, they were variable symbols. And I stopped at 3, but we're going to have a 3, a 4, a 5, a 6, many variable symbols, as many as you want.

Alright, so let's repeat what these things are. Parentheses, and then not, AND, OR, some of you haven't seen these symbols before, so they are new, they are not only in logic. So the not is like a negation, like a minus, but with a little leg. AND, OR are the V and the backward V. if, then is the arrow, if AND only if. And then variable symbols, which is not only 3, we can use as many as we want.

A1, A2, A3, A4, A5, A6. They are variable symbols, and they are going to represent sentences, which may be true or false, depending on the situation. Okay, so we have the symbols. Now we have to figure out how to put them together, right, to make sentences. So we are going to put them together in what we call expressions. So an expression is going to be a string of symbols, so many of these symbols put together in a way that makes sense.

At first, you are going to see the string of symbols, and you are going to be able to tell which ones make sense and which ones don't. But we need a formal definition for that. Here is an example of an expression. A1 implies A2, and A1 implies A2. Yeah, so this is something that, an expression that makes sense, is going to be a valid expression in another language.

So this is good. And, but for instance, we can have other strings, parentheses, parentheses, imply A1, if AND only if, if AND only if, which are just crazy. I mean, this makes no sense, and this is not in the language. only some expressions are going to be valid, right? So this one on top is valid, and the one in the bottom is not. So only some strings, that's very, are going to be valid.

**And we need a formal definition to be able to tell, in a formal way, which ones are VALID expressions, and which ones are NOT.** Okay.

Okay, so these valid expressions are going to be called well-formed formulas. Okay, so these expressions are sometimes called formulas and the ones that are valid are going to be called well-formed. Okay, so let's look at the definition. **What is the definition of the well-formed formulas?** The well-formed formulas are going to be strings which are built in one of the following ways.

First one is all the variables a1, a2, a3, all of them by themselves they form a well-formed formula. Okay, so a single variable by itself is a well-formed formula. And then if you have two well-formed formulas that we built before. Let's call this a phi and psi. These are formulas that we have built already. And then from them we can build new well-formed formulas. So which ones we can build? We can build not phi, that would be a well-formed formula.

We can build phi and psi. We can build phi or psi. We can build phi implies psi. And we can build phi if and only is psi. Alright, so we build these well-formed formulas from the bottom up. Start with the variable symbols and then you attach the nodes and or implies if and only is to get larger formulas. Okay, let's look at some examples. For instance, if you write the variable a3 by itself, that's a well-formed formula.

Then if you write the formula a5, the variable a5, that's a well-formed formula. And then you can put them together with an and symbol. And that's now you have a more composed well-formed formula. And then you can have a bigger well-formed formula, putting it right there. And then you can extend that one by putting an or and then attaching it to another well-formed formula. That could be a1 if AND only if a2.

Alright, so that's how we build them up. From the bottom up you get the well-formed formulas. Okay, that's how we define the language of sentential logic. Next time we're going to look at truth values. See you next time.

## 4. Part p04 - Truth Assignments

So in this video we're going to be talking about truce assignments. So we saw in the previous video how to define a language for sentential logic, right? So we had a way of writing these things that we call formulas. These formulas that we call well-formed formulas that were built out of variables using and, ors, nots, etc. And where the variables were representing sentences, propositions. So the variables could be either true or false and then out of them will be the larger formula or sentence. Now we're going to see how to assign truth values to these formulas, alright? So the first thing we need to do to assign a truth value to a formula is assign truth values to the variables. What I want to do is assign to.

Each formula a truth value, right? So a value that could be either true or false. So these truth values, as I was saying, they could be either T or F. That's what we're going to use for true or false. Okay? So only two values. Sometimes you could think of them as 0 or 1, yes or no, whatever you want. Essentially there are two values. One represents truth and one represents false. Okay? So we want to assign these values to each formula. Let's look at an example. Okay, consider the formula P or not Q. Okay? So here P and Q are not Q. So is this true or false? What do you guys think? Well, to tell whether it's true or false, we need to know whether P and Q are true or false, right? Well, the answer to this is going to depend.

On the truth values of P and Q, right? We couldn't know whether P or not Q is true or false without knowing about P and Q before, right? So the truth value of a formula is going to depend on the truth values of its variables. So we're going to define a way such as depending on the truth values of the variables in a well formed formula, we're going to assign a truth value to the well formed formula.

We're going to see how to do this in detail, right? Remember that well formed formula is one of those formulas that are built using the rules we have for formulas in the last video. But they are the ones that make sense, right? The ones that you can write. Okay, so how are we going to do this? So let S be a set of variable symbols. Okay? So for instance, S could be the symbols a1, a2, dot, dot, dot, dot, up to a sub k. So then we define a truth assignment for S.

It's nothing more than a function that assigns to each variable symbol in the set S at truth value, right? So it's a function that goes from S, so takes one of those values, coming from either a1, a2, a k, assigns to each of those a truth value. That's a T or an S, right? Okay, let's see an example. Suppose S is very simple. S just consists of three variable symbols, a1, a2, and a3. That's it. So only those three variables symbols. Now, for example, we may have v of a1 to be true, v of a2 to be false, and v of a3 to be, let's say, false, right? So there's a variable assignment. We assign a truth assignment, sorry. We assign a truth value to each variable.

All right? So there's not much to this. So now the next step is to assign the truth values to formulas in general, not just the variables. All right? So let's continue with the example. So let's suppose that with this assignment right here, we want to assign a truth value to the following formula. A1 or not A2. What would be the truth value of that formula, given our previous truth assignment for the formulas for the variables A1 and A2? Well, it has to be true. Why is that?

Because A1 was assigned truth, right? That's right here. And A2 was assigned false, but the negation of A2 is then true. And if you have an or at this junction of two things that are true, then you're going to get a true answer, right? So that's how you get that. Let's look at another example. Suppose now we want to look at the truth value of A1 or A3 altogether and A2.

What would be the truth value of this? Well, we have to do a method that's similar to the one we just did. Let's look at the truth value of each particular variable and then they'll use the whole truth value. So A2, according to our assumption right here, is false. So we should put an F right there. And then we have that A1 right here is true and A3 right here is false.

Now these two together form a disjunction. One is true, one is false. So all together is true. One of the two is true. That's what disjunction means. One of those two is true. So we get true and then we get a true and a false and that's it. For to have an and we have to have both to be true. So we get that that sentence is false, given the particular truth assignment of the variable sentence. Okay, so now what we need to do is define this procedure, which I think you mostly understood how to do intuitively, but define it precisely and in general. So the idea is that whenever we have a truth assignment for all the variables in a certain set, we can extend the truth assignment to all the formulas.

That use those variables, right? So we have V that takes each variable in the set S to either a T or an F, we want to extend it to a truth assignment that we call V bar. And now V bar is not defined just in S, but it's defined in all of S bar, where S bar is a set of all the formulas, the well-formed formulas that you can write using only the variables in S.

S. Example, suppose that S is just a set A1, A2, and A3. All right, same as the example before. So what are the formulas we can write using these three variables? Well, there are infinitely many. Let me just list a few so you get the idea. First, remember the variables alone, they are formulas also, right? So you can, so those three A1, A2, A3 by themselves are formulas. But then you also have not A1, not A2, not A3. Those are three more formulas you write with them. And then you can combine them. A1 and A2. A1 and A3. A1 and not A2. A2 or not A3. And so on and so forth. And you can start building everything with ands, ors, implies, even only if and nots from those formulas out. All right,.

So that's S bar. Everything you can build with A1 and A2 and A3. Let's go back to our definition. So we want to now define this extension on the set S bar. Now we want to extend the truth values to the whole set F bar with all the formulas now that use the ones in S. So how are we going to do it? Well, we're going to do this by recursion. On what? Recursion on the size of the well-formed formula, of the formula. So that means we're going to start defining V bar on the small formulas and then start building up to all the formulas. Let phi be a well-formed formula. We want to define the value of V bar on phi. And that's going to depend on the shape of phi, right? For instance, if phi is a.

Sentential variable, so that's kind of the simplest case we have for phi, then V bar applied to phi is going to be the same as V, right? So if you start with a variable, well, we already know how to assign truth values to the variables. So all we do is just copy, all right? So that's right there. V bar of phi is the same as V of phi. V of phi is the one we started with and from V, we are building V bar, right? Okay, so now let's suppose phi is a bit more interesting.

Now let's suppose phi is of the form not psi. Okay, so psi is another well-formed formula that we built before. It's a smaller formula because it doesn't have the symbol not. So it's a smaller formula than phi. So let's assume that we already know how to assign a truth value to psi. then V bar of phi is going to be the opposite of the value of V bar of psi, right? V bar of psi, we calculated before, we know what the truth value is. And now if we look at not psi, the truth value is going to be the opposite. All right, so let's write that in a more formal way. So V bar of not psi is going to be true if V bar of psi is false and it's going to be false if V bar of psi is true, right? The opposite.

Okay, and now we're going to find in the same way, we're going to define what happens with the other connected. So if you have a formula that is phi and psi, now phi and psi is bigger than phi by itself and psi by itself, and we already defined the truth value of phi and the truth value of psi, then what do we get? That the truth value of phi and psi is true if both V of V bar of phi and V bar of psi are true, right? So with the AND symbol both need to be true to get a true.

Now let's look at what happens with this junction. So if we consider V bar of phi or psi, what are we going to get? We're going to get true if one of the values of V bar of phi or V bar of psi are true, right? So for the OR, which is that little symbol, the V symbol, you need only one of them to be true to get a true. What's up with implication? if you have V bar of phi implies psi, it's going to be true if either V bar of phi is false or V bar of psi is true. Okay, that's a typo right there.

So I mean, you get true if V bar of phi is false or V bar of psi is true. So the true and the f are wrong there. I'll make a little comment about this in a second. Let's go to if AND only if. So V bar of phi if AND only if psi is going to be true. if AND only if the true values of phi and psi are the same. So if they are both true, you get true. if they are both false, you get true.

But if they are different, you get false. Okay? Okay, so let's look at that implication case that was, they had a little typo and it was a bit, a bit strange. So V bar of phi implies psi is going to be false. And that's what we have to remember. When is this false? When is the implication false? Well, the implication is going to be false if the first one, phi is true, but psi is false, right? So in that case, we know phi does not imply psi, right? So let me repeat that. Phi implies psi is going to be false when phi is true, but psi is false, right? And in all the other cases, phi implies psi is true.

So it's a bit weird because here in the case when phi is false, the first one is false, like false implies false, we are saying that's true, essentially because there is no contradiction. So an implication means if this is true, then that's true, right? So it's only going to be false if the first one is true, but the latter one is not true. Yeah? Pretty good? We can write these definitions in table form, right? So we can remember like all of what's there, but in one table, okay? So how do we do this? So on one side, we put the truth values, the possible truth values of phi and psi, right? So here we have phi and psi, and each one can be either true or false, right? So we have four combinations,.

True, true, true, false, false, true, and false, false. Those are the four combinations, right? And now in each of those combinations, we're going to decide what is the truth value of the composed formulas we get from them. So the first one, the negation, we did that one before, we have to reverse the case for phi, right? So this column here is reversing the fifth column, all right? So the first column, phi is the first one. So we're just reversing that. When it's true, we get false. Now when we look at the conjunction, the end, this little symbol is the upside down v, is the end. We get that this one is true if AND only if both of them are true. So that's the only the top row. When we look at the.

Or, the v-shape, this junction, this junction is true if AND only if one of them is true. So the first three rows are true, and the last one is the one that is false. In the implies case, let's do it correctly now, phi implies psi is going to be false if AND only if the first one is true and the other one is false. And all the other cases are true, all right? So that's what we get up there.

And then finally, we have if AND only if, phi if and only psi is going to be true. We say only when both of them are the same value. So if we get both truths, we're going to put a true right there and if both false, we put a true right there and the other cases are false. Okay? ## 6. Part p06 - Completeness of the Language for Sentential Logic

Welcome everybody! So today we're going to be looking at connectives and the question we're going to be looking at is are they enough? So is it possible that we may want to say something logically that we couldn't say with these connectives? It's an important question to ask. So let's suppose, let's start with an example.

Let's suppose we have a new connective that we call SHARP and what it does is takes the majority vote. So this is a SHARP, it's a connective among three things. So this formula and this SHARP is tautologically equivalent to the formula phi and psi or phi and theta or theta and psi, right? So these two of them are true. That uses only the connectives or and not. Okay? So only with the connectives or and not. That's it. not and, not even only it, not implies. Only those two connectives. You can write a formula that is going to represent the formula if that you're given. Yeah? So this is quite something. So you can represent all k-placed boolean functions only with and and or. You don't need anything else. Here's the proof.

So we already know that there is a well-formed formula. Let's call it alpha tilde, alpha tilde, whose boolean function is the f. This alpha tilde might use the connectives and, well, in the proof we did before we only use ands and ors and nots. We didn't use anything else. But okay, let's suppose this one uses anything. And what we're going to do is just replace each occurrence of if AND only if inside alpha tilde for this formula. So whenever you see an if AND only if, you replace it for this bigger formula. And then we keep on going. So now we now we have a formula that has no if AND only if. Now we replace each occurrence of implication of phi implied psi by.

Not phi or psi. Which remember this is equivalent to phi implied psi. So now we just replace each one for this and now we have a formula that has no implications. So no if AND only if and no implications. And now finally we replace each and by not phi or not psi. And now we end up with a formula that has no ands either. We end up with a formula that only has nots and ors and is equivalent to the alpha tilde that we started with. And the alpha tilde that we started with was representing the function that we have.

So all functions, all boolean functions can be represented only with or and ands. All right, so that's it for today. Or it's for this video. See you guys next time.

## 7. Part p07 - Generating sets out of functions

## 8. Part p08 - Structural induction and Recursion

Okay, welcome back everybody. So now let's continue with our closure of sets and now let's just prove a couple of important properties or techniques that we use on these closures and the two techniques are induction and recursion. So induction refers to when you want to prove something by induction and recursion is when you want to build something by recursion using what you built so far, you build the new things.

Okay, so induction means you're proving something, you assume you proved the previous cases and you prove the next and recursion, you assume you built the previous cases and you built the next. So what is the setting for the induction principle? So you start with a set C generated by certain family functions starting from a certain set B and by applying the assumptions. Okay, so I now consider a property B. This is a property you want to show that every element has this property B and what is the assumption?

First, you start all the starting points, all the ones in B satisfy the property B and then the other property is that whenever you have a tuple of elements that you know satisfies property B, then if you apply any of the functions in F, the image also satisfies the property B. And then from here you want to conclude that everybody is at a size B. So let's look at an example before we go to the proof. So let's just look at the case of the natural numbers.

So this is the example from the previous video where we get that the closure is exactly the natural numbers inside all the real numbers. And let's say you want to prove something by induction of the natural numbers. So what do you do when you want to prove something in induction of the natural numbers? Let's just state induction for the natural numbers and see if it makes, if it's the same thing as the one we have here.

So it says the following. if we know that C satisfies the property B and that whenever a number, we conclude that every natural number N satisfies the property B. Right? That's what induction says. if you know it's for zero and you know it for N plus one every time you know it for N, then you can deduce that you know it for all natural numbers. Right? So that's exactly what we're going to do here. And the natural numbers are a particular case of this thing that we are saying right now.

So that's exactly what we're going to do. But now we're going to do it in the general case of a closure of a bunch of functions starting from a bunch of elements. Okay? So let's see what is the general statement that we want. So, well, that's the statement. So we have the setting, all the elements of B satisfy B and if C does, so does F of C.

And we want to show that everybody in C satisfies B. Okay? So what is the proof of this? **Well, what we want to show the claim is that the set, let's call it Roman B, is the set of all the things, all the elements, let's call them little u and u in our universe, that satisfy B, that that set is inductive. IF B is inductive, THEN this C MUST be included in the same, because IF so, C will be included in this set, B, and we will have that everybody in C satisfies the property, calligraphic B.**

Okay, so let's see why that's the case. Why is that this set is inductive? **Well, all elements of B satisfy the property, so that means that B is included in P.** Okay, so that's the first part of being inductive. And if C satisfies B, so that's F of C for all the functions that we have in our family, so we get that B is closed. Right? So essentially, right, so these two say B is inductive. And if it's inductive, then C has to be a subset of it. So very simple.

So then if you want to prove something about terms, all we have to do is first prove it about the variables, and then prove that whenever we know it's true for certain formulas, then it's going to be true for whatever we get by applying the connectives to them. Okay, so let's look at recursion. We have the same setting. **And now this time, we want to just simplify the setting a little bit, just to be able to state the theorem better, because otherwise, it gets too messy, the notation.**

But from this version, you should be able to define, like, think of the general case, you just extend this to the general case. So this should be general enough, but maybe simpler to write. So in this case, we have two functions. So F takes two inputs, and G takes only one input. Okay, so, and then we have a set B. And the idea is that we need, you want to define functions by recursion.

So in the example of the natural numbers that we did before, what does it mean to define a function by recursion? **Sometimes we define a function, let's say, like the function factorial.** How do we define factorial? You say, well, zero factorial is equal to one, and n plus one factorial is equal to n, multiplied by n factorial, multiplied by n plus one. Have you seen a definition like this before? So this is a recursive definition. So we define it at zero, and first, and then whenever we define it at n plus one, using the value that we had at n. So that's the definition by recursion. We use the previous one to define the next value.

Okay, so in our general setting of closed sets, we need to assume one more thing, and is that the set is generated freely. freely. **Freely means that the set B and the image of F and the image of G are all disjoint.** So that means that no terms get confused, right? So this means equivalently, means that for every little c in the set C, it can be built only in one way.

So either C belongs to B, and it was at the beginning, or C is equal to F of some tuple S for some unique S in C, less than omega, or C equals G of S for some unique S in less than omega. Okay, so that's what it means to be fully generated. **We should add to the definition that they are disjoint, and F and G are one to one.**

So there's only one way to build things. So every element either comes from F, or comes from G, or starts from the beginning. And if it comes from F, you know where it came from. if you come from G, you know where it came from. For instance, in the case of terms, it's very important that the terms, the well found formulas are generated freely. Because if you want, if you have a well found formula, you want to know how was it built, right?

So if you see in that example, if you see the formula, then you know this was built by applying the implication on A1 or A2 and A3. Right? That's the only way to get this formula here from applying the operation. The only way to apply it is using the implication. So that's why it's free. It's no other way. if you didn't have the parentheses though, it wouldn't be freely generated.

Because if you didn't have the parentheses, and if you have A1 or A2 implies A3. Well, maybe this comes from an application of implication, but also maybe this is application of OR of two things, right? It could be the OR of this and the OR of this guy, separated. So you wouldn't know how you build it. if you first did the OR or first you did the implies, and it wouldn't be free, and you wouldn't know how to read the formula.

So it's very important that they are freely generated, the formulas. The same with the natural numbers. Okay, so freely generated means it's a unique way to build each single element. So let's go to recursion. So recursion says the following. Suppose that C is freely generated. And now, so we consider a little function H that is defined on our set V. So here we have our set C and inside is V.

And here is our set V. And we have, what do we have? We have a function here that comes from here to here. That's little h. Okay, it's only defined in V. And now we have two functions, capital F and capital G. And they go inside here. So F takes two inputs and takes one. This is F. And G takes one input and output one. All right.

And our C is the set generated by F. So inside here we have our function little f. And we have our function G. All right.

Those are inside the C side. And now we're given capital F and capital G on the V side. Okay.

So that's our given, right? So we're given is, let's put them in black. We're given is this H, this F and this G. And now what we want to do is extend this bar up here. Extend this H to something called H bar. That is defined everywhere on C. Because the previous H was defined only on the V part. Now we want to define it everywhere on C.

And we want to use this capital F and capital G to tell us how to do it. Well, so H on B, F is going to be H bar and H are the same. So that's why it's an extension. And then every element of C, since it's freely generated, is being generated, it either comes from B or is generated by F or is generated by G. Right?

So it's one of those three. There is nothing else. So if it's generated by F, F has two inputs. That's our assumption right here. if it's generated by F, what we want H bar to be of this guy, is what we get by applying capital F to this guy and this guy. Right? And if it's generated by G, we want to see what H bar did on little x, and then apply capital G.

Okay? So notice that, so essentially here you have the term F of x, y, that's been built one step after x and y. So suppose we know already what H bar is on x and y, and using the values that we know about H bar of x and H bar of y, we build the value of H bar on F, x, y. Yeah? So we build the value on this complicated term using the values on the simpler terms and our function capital F.

**So what's an example of such a definition by recursion?** So we saw one already. So the definition of the truth assignment was exactly by induction like that. by recursion like this, right? So we started with a valuation V that was a formula that went, that was a function that went from the set of variables to true-false. And then we wanted to extend it to a truth assignment that goes from all well-formed formulas to a true-false.

Right? And we said, well, in the variables, the V and V bar do exactly the same thing. And then you have to see what it does on the bigger formulas. And then to define on the bigger formulas, you use the previous one. Right? So if you remember, if you had, if you wanted to define V bar on a formula that is of the form phi and psi, then what do you do?

Well, you use the fact that you know what it is on phi and you know what it is on psi, and then you apply. So that's exact, that is, if you guys remember what these guys was, B of the AND, you guys remember what this is? Maybe, I guess the way we wrote it before was A1 and A2. And this function, additive two, and you apply to V bar of phi and V bar of psi. Right? So if both of them are true, you get true. if one of them is false, you get false. And then, so you're using the previous values to get the new value. Like here, you're using the previous value to get the new value. Okay? ## 9. Part p09 - The Language of First-Order Logic

All right, welcome everybody. So today we're gonna start with first the language of first-order logic. So first-order logic is strong enough to develop all of mathematics. It's much stronger than sentential logic. Still, as we're gonna see, it's not super complicated. This language is not that complicated. Still, but it's not restrictive at all in the sense that we, one can develop all mathematics. One can write every mathematical statement in this language if you are in the right setting.

Like, for instance, the setting of set theory. So today we just start with describing what the language is, and as you see, it's quite simple. All right, so we need to define two things to develop a language. One is the symbols, and the other one is how to put the symbols together, right? So our expressions are gonna be nothing else but strings of symbols. Like when you write on a computer and you have an email, it's a string of characters.

Or a program is a string of characters. So our mathematical expressions are gonna be also strings of symbols. And that we can only put together according to certain rules, like the same as in English. All right, so let's start with the symbols, okay? So let's slowly go to the symbols that we have, and then we'll move on to explain how you put them together. Okay, so there are different kind of symbols.

So first come the logical symbols, and there are a few of them. The logical symbols are always included in first of all the logics. First one is the symbol for equality. Some people don't put it, but yes, we're gonna always consider equality. The equality symbol that you guys, I assume, know very well. then come the connectives, ifs that have the logical meanings. Let's write down what they are.

So this one is and, this one is or, this one is if AND only if, this one is implies, this one is not, for all, and that exists. Those are our logical symbols. then come the variable symbols. So we're gonna be able to use variables in our formulas. The variables, formally, we call them v0, v1, v2, v3, v4. And we can use as many variables as we want in each formula.

Finally, many in each formula. But overall, it depends. We can choose as many formulas as we want. In general, we are gonna use, just because visually, it looks better if we use variables x, y, z instead of v0, v1, v2. It's all like a lot of these together. But formally, we think that we are using this v0, v1, v2. Okay, equality, connect these variables symbols. What else?

We have punctuation symbols. And we're only gonna use the parentheses. So the left parentheses and the right parentheses are characters that can show up in our formula. And they are only gonna help us to be able to parse the formula. To like read it and understand. And not to get confused about how to read the formula. Okay, so these are the logical symbols. They are in all settings, whenever you're in first-order logic.

And then come the vocabulary symbols. The vocabulary symbols, they vary according to the situation. So it depends what you're working on. You can have different vocabulary symbols. All right, so they are not always the same. Let's just view a few examples of settings. So for instance, if you're talking about groups, you have usually three symbols. One lambda e for the identity, multiplication or addition or just some of the operation symbol.

So there are three types of vocabulary symbols. Let's go through them one by one. **So we have these, the C's are called the constant symbols.** The constant symbols, they represent elements. So that's the difference between relation symbols and function symbols. Functions return objects, relations return true or false. Let's notice that, so both function and relation symbols can take arbitrary numbers of inputs, but each symbol has a fixed additive. All right.

## 11. Part p11 - Free Variables

Is not bound to any quantifier. Let me give you an example to see what that means. So suppose you have a formula of the form. It's for every other W.

So if these things happen. Right? So that's how those are bound. And then we have Y. Y here, here and here is free. So it's not bound to any quantifier. Okay? So that's the same in this case. Y up here and here and here. That variable is free while the X is bound to that existential right there. When we say X is X we mean that X refers to that existential.

And then when we have a well form formula that has no free variables. We are going to call that a sentence. Okay? Because we have the Y is bound to this Y. X is bound to this X. And everybody is bounded. So there are no free variables.

That means this one up here is a sentence. Sentences have no free variables. And in that case we have no problem figuring out whether the sentence was true or false. Because since the variables are bound we know that they mean. We know what they mean by when we write X and Y. So there were no free variables. In the case of free variables that's when we cannot give a direct answer. Because the answer is going to depend on the value of the free variable. Right?

So in this case we couldn't give a direct answer. Because the answer to whether these things are true or false. Depends on the value of Y. if we need the value of Y to tell whether these are true or false. It's not just plain. So if you know a sentence, it's not that immediate to tell if something is true or false. Okay. So let me, let's define formally the set of free variables.

And let's use what we learned in the previous video about induction and recursion to define this. Okay. So the well-formed formulas can be defined. They are all strings of symbols. And they can define as a closure, as a closure of these operations. So putting the ands, the formulas together by ands, by connectives, ors, implies, if AND only if. So we need to define the set of free variables using, essentially, induction. So first we want to define a function, let's call it var, that goes from the set of terms to the set of variables. Okay? T1 up to tk, so k terms because f has added tk. So that means you apply to k terms. And these terms were built before. So these are terms that are already strings of symbols that were built before. ## 12. Part p12 - Interpretation of Terms

And the idea is that phi sub n is going to say that the structure has at least n elements. Okay, where, so it's going to say there is at least n elements, so it's going to say x1, x2, up to xn, such that they're all different.

So we have to put all the combinations of x1, x2, x3, up to xn, and say that they are all different. x1 different than x2, and x2 different than x3, x3 different than x4, and all of them. All the possible combinations of these variables, so that they are all different. So this formula phi sub n says there exists n different things. So the universe of the structure has to have at least n elements or more. So if you put them all of them together for all the n, the only way to have that is if you have an infinite structure. All right, so this gamma right here is an example of an infinite list of axioms, which you cannot reduce to finitely many, and that requires.

A little proof, whose models are the infinite groups. So I'm showing that the infinite groups are a weekly elementary class. And they are not going to be the groups by itself, like a class of all groups containing finite and infinite, those are an elementary class. But if you want to restrict yourself to the infinite, you need to add infinitely many more axioms, making it a weekly elementary class.

All right, we'll see more of these in the next videos. See you guys later.

## 18. Part p18 - Homomorphisms

Welcome back everybody. So today we're going to look at the notions of homomorphisms, embeddings, isomorphisms, automorphisms, all like kind of morphisms from one structure to another. So this is the notion most of you guys, well, I hope all of you guys remember from algebra where you saw some examples essentially, some particular cases of these guys for particular classes of structures. For instance, here's one example. if you guys remember a homomorphism of groups from a group G to a group H. So here the E and the I are the identity of the groups.

The E is for the G and the I is for H. So those are the identity elements. **And let's say this one has a multiplication operation that is called star and this one has an operation that is called all times.** So a homomorphism from G to H is a map, it's a function from G to H, which maps identity to the identity and which preserves the operation.

And you have that whenever you have G1 and G2 in G and you multiply them and here is G1 star G2, you get the same thing on the other side. So if here is H of G1 and H of G2, then when you multiply them, you get the corresponding guy. All right. So that means if you multiply before applying H or you multiply after applying H, it doesn't matter. You get the same results, right? So notice this operation here is in the G side while this operation here is in H side because because these guys belong to H.

Good. Okay. So that's a homomorphism of groups, which I hope you guys remember from abstract algebra. And for instance, let's look at a few examples of these guys just to see what these guys were. So let's take the group Z with zero and plus and the group of Z2 of zero plus. Well, if you guys remember Z2 is the group that only contains two elements, zero and one.

And we can define H from here to here by defining H of N equals one if N is odd and zero if N is even. So this is a homomorphism of group. So the zero gets mapped to zero. And if you add two elements, if you add two integers, why is it that preserved on the other side? Well, because if you add two even integers, which means we should give you zero, you're going to get zero. You're going to even, which is zero. if you add two odd integers that give you one, you should get even. So about one plus one here is zero and so on and so forth.

One even plus one odd is going to give you an odd. So zero plus a one is going to give you one. So you can check all the combinations and see that this is actually a homomorphism of groups. Just a reminder. The same story was for other structures. For instance, homomorphism of rings. And here in the ring, we have more structure. Now we have a zero and a one and a plus and a times.

And in the other ring, we have a zero that we call O and a unity that we call E and an O plus and an O times. These are two rings. They could be anything like the real or the rationals or the complex numbers or the ring of polynomials. And they satisfy that to be a homomorphism of rings, you need to satisfy that zero goes, the zero of one goes to the zero of the other. The identity for multiplication of one goes to the entity of multiplication of the other. And that both operations are preserved. A plus goes to plus times goes to times. Right? So that's a homomorphism of rings. So it preserves all the structure, essentially. if you have order rings, for instance, which I guess this one you haven't.

Seen in algebra. Well, you also have an order operation, an order relation, sorry. Ordering is a relation among the elements. Like for instance, the integers with the usual ordering or the reals with the usual orderings. then on top of having a homomorphism of rings, you need to have that the ordering is preserved. Right? So that would be a homomorphism of order rings. It preserves also the order. When you map two things, if A is if G one is less than G two, when you map them, you get something less than the other.

Preserving the order. Okay, so we can now imagine how to generalize this definition to all structures. **And so here is the definition, which is a, it's a bit long, but ONLY because we are just trying to do it in full generality.** But there isn't that much to it. This is not as essentially a generalization of this, but done, no, just taking care of all possibilities. So, okay, so let's suppose we have a vocabulary tau. A vocabulary tau has some constant symbols, some function symbols, and some relation symbols, right? In this case, tau had two constant symbols, two function symbols, and one relation symbol. Let's just consider the general case. And now, now let's take two tau structures, m and n. So, m has domain m, and n has domain n. And we have.

Interpretations. To be tau structures, we need interpretations for the constants, functions, and relations on the vocabulary. And we are going to call them like this. So, the interpretation of the constant symbol c1, we're going to denote it by c1 superscript m in the m structure and superscript n on the n structure. Okay, and the same for f, it's for the function symbols and the relation symbols. So, essentially what we have here is, so f, let's say that one there, f1 is a function symbol to a character, and f1 superscript m is not a symbol. This one is a function from n to whatever the identity is of that function to m. Okay, so maybe we put the superscript m, we're not talking about the symbol anymore.

Now we're talking about the actual function representing the symbol in this particular structure. All right, so we say that a homomorphism from m to n is going to be a function, again h, from defined everywhere in the domain of m to n, which preserves all this structure. So what do we mean by preserve all this structure? For every constant symbol in m is going to be mapped to the corresponding constant symbol, interpretation of the constant symbol in m. For every function, for every function symbol, when we look at the interpretation in m, if we apply it to a bunch of elements in m, and then we apply h, that's the same thing as first applying h, and then the function symbol. All right, so for instance, what I'm saying here.

Here is that if you have elements m1 up to mk in the structure m, when we apply h, we map them to now n, right? So h goes from m to n, so we get a bunch of elements of n, and now we're saying that if you apply f on each of the two sides, here we apply f as interpreting the structure m, and here we apply f interpreting the structure n, which is their two different functions, they belong to different structures, but what we get is that the corresponding values that we get are also, of course, they also match through h. Okay, so if you apply h first and then f, or f and then h, we get the same result. Okay, so that's what it means to preserve the structure. That.

This function on m, when you commute it with the homomorphism, you know you have the interpretation in n. And the same story for the relations. if you interpret a tuple belongs to the interpretation in m of r, you can only if the image of that tuple, through h, belongs to the interpretation of that relation in n. Okay, so those are essentially these things up here. So this is the case for functions. if you apply plus first and then h, it's the same as if you compare these two elements according to the ordering in g, it's the same as comparing the images of these elements under the ordering in h. Right, so that means the whole structure is preserved. So that's the homomorphism. Every, every, um, every structure, every...

Okay, so there are some, uh, so there are some special kinds of homomorphisms that are going to be very useful. Homomorphisms by itself, by itself, uh, won't be that useful for the class. Um, but embeddings will. So an embedding is a homomorphism that is one to one. That's the only problem. And actually, if you think about what it means to be one to one. **One to one means that m1 equals m2 IF AND ONLY IF h of m1 equals h of m2.**

Right? That's what it means for h to be one to one. **So essentially saying one to one means that you preserve equality in m.** This is in the structure m and this is in the structure n. You preserve equality, which is equality could be seen as a relationship. And here we are saying that we need to preserve all relations. We didn't say anything about equality. In embeddings, we also have to preserve equality. And of course, that's something that we want, uh, for the, for the homomorphisms that we're going to be considering. And of course, that's something that we want for, to have nice properties.

Okay, so if an embedding is also onto, that means it's a bijection, we say that it's an isomorphism. Okay, so an isomorphism is an embedding, uh, that is also onto. And, uh, let me remark that the textbook uses isomorphic embedding to just, what I want to just call embedding, plain embedding. And, uh, for me and actually for everybody in algebra, um, isomorphism is the word you use when you have a bijection. So you preserve the structure and you preserve it essentially back and forth.

Another special kind is automorphisms. So an automorphism is an isomorphism from a structure to itself. Okay, so, and they're going to be very useful. We're going to see that, uh, in a minute. So let me, um, mention, um, a few examples of isomorphisms. I mentioned this one in class the other day. So let's suppose we have the group R zero plus, and we're going to map this to R R one times. ## 20. Part p20 - Non-definability results using automorphisms

## 21. Part p21 - Substructures

Welcome back everybody. So now I want to mention a notion that is very much connected to that of embedding and elementary embedding, but it's simpler. And it's that of substructure. if you remember in your algebra class you looked at subspaces for vector spaces. Those are substructures. I don't know if you remember subrings when you had a ring. It's a smaller ring that is inside. So in general we're going to generalize that and we call them a substructure. So here's the definition. So if you have two structures m and n and they are tau structures, so they are the same vocabulary, use the same symbols, we say that m is a substructure of n. Essentially pretty much m is included in n and everything else behaves the same way in m and in n. All right? So the constants, the way you interpret the constant.

Symbols in m is the same as the way you interpret the constant symbols in n. The way you interpret the function symbols in m is the same as the way you interpret the function symbols in n and the same for the relation. So for instance, let's say that we take substructure z which is the integers with 0, 1, plus, times, and less than. Okay? And we get calligraphic r is the reals with 0, same structure plus, times, less than, but now we are talking about the plus and times of the reals. So it's not hard to see that z is a substructure of r, right? It's a subset and all the operations are the same. This, we are talking about the same 0, the same 1, plus in the integers it's just a restriction. What this symbol means here.

Is that the restriction of this function plus on the reals to the integers. So we, so that means like this is, this is the plus, this is, these are the same operations but this one only takes integer value. So this is a restriction of this one just to the integers. And here is the same. It's a multiplication for the integers like the one for the reals but only takes integers. So that's why it's the restriction.

And less than on the integers, this one is used to compare integers, but it's the same relation that you use to compare reals just restricted to the integers. Okay? So that's what that means. So that's what a substructure is. So it's, it's a, and, and one thing that is very important, substructures, these operations, these functions need to be closed within the substructure. **This is implicit in the definition because we have here that z has to be a structure itself, well like the m here in the definition has to be a substructure itself.** So to be a substructure, all the functions need to be defined inside of you. So you need to be closed on the functions, right? So when you apply the functions inside the substructure, you must stay inside the substructure,.

Right? **So in general, IF you guys remember, for instance, IF you have a group, g is a group, let's say g, e star, it's a group, and THEN we define a subgroup.** So h, usually you say h is fine, in g is a subgroup, if two things, e belongs to h, the identity, and for all h1 and h2 in h, h1 star h2 belongs to h. That's the usual definition in algebra for subgroup, right? So that means that it's closed under the operation. So what we mean here is that if you look at h as a structure itself, e comes with the same operation, what this is saying is that it's actually a structure, e belongs to h, and h is closed under the operation. So when we consider h with this.

## 22. Part p22 - Compactness

Welcome everybody. So now we're going to talk about compactness. Compactness is one of the most important theorems about first-order logic. It talks about the properties of the logic itself. Alright, so we said that in this course we're going to, instead of looking at the foundation of all of mathematics, we are analyzing this particular logic, which is used in other studies to do the foundations of mathematics, but we are going to look at properties of this logic. So things that we can derive from the facts that we can use first-order logic in so many ways. And compactness, as we're going to see in many applications, it's a very important one. Before we go into the statements, let's review a notion that we defined, I think, last week, that says the following. So if.

You have a set of sentences gamma, so these are sentences of first-order logic, and say, let's say in some vocabulary tau. So a set of sentences, imagine it's infinite, or finite, doesn't matter, actually. We say that it's satisfiable if there exists one model M that satisfies all the sentences in gamma. There exists a tau structure M, such that M satisfies all the formulas that are in gamma. Okay, so that's what it means to satisfy. So one structure that makes all the sentences in gamma true.

Okay, so that's for a whole set to be satisfied. And compactness says the following. if you have a set of sentences, think of an infinite set, then if every finite subset of gamma is satisfiable, then gamma is satisfied. Okay, so that's what it says. So only just by knowing that the finite subset of a set are satisfiable, then the whole thing is satisfied. The first thing to notice is why it's not trivial. And the reason is that if a finite subset, if you have a finite subset satisfiable here, another finite subset satisfiable here, that means there is a model that makes it true, there is another model that makes it true, and there's another model that makes it true. So every finite subset may be satisfied.

By a different model. I mean, the fact that it's satisfiable, it only says that it's satisfied by some model, but it might be a different for each finite subset, right? So there is no reason to believe from there that there is going to be one structure that is going to satisfy all of them at the same time, right? **In principle, it could be different for the different structures, for the different subsets.** So let's look at an example. It's a very interesting example where it shows precisely this. Okay, so in this example, we have the vocabulary of essentially of a ring, an order ring, which we're going to use for the natural numbers, semi-ring, and a constant symbol, another constant symbol that we're going to call c.

Okay, so we add a constant symbol to the vocabulary of the natural numbers. And then we're going to consider t to be the following set of sentences. First, we take the theory of the natural numbers as an order of semi-ring. So we see the one plus times and less than without using the constant c. Okay, so only in the standard natural numbers. Remember, the theory of a structure means instead of all the sentences, not the formulas, without three variables, sentences, sentences that are true in this structure. Okay, so take everything, every first order sentence you can write in this vocabulary that is true about the natural numbers. Okay, so including Fermat's last theorem, everything.

And then add to that a whole collection of sentences that we call phi sub p, where p is a prime number, where phi sub p is the following sentence. There exists an x such that x times 1 plus 1 plus 1 equals c. What does that say? You can see what that says. This thing says essentially that p divides c, right? But it's just written in the language that we have with 0, 1 and times and yeah. So that's what phi sub p says.

Okay, so what do we have here? We have, so we put all these formulas together. And essentially, if you put all the formulas together, what you will need is that whoever is, c is a constant number, it's being interpreted. if you want a model for it, it has to be interpreted somewhat. So it has to be interpreted by some number that is a multiple of all the prime numbers. And this is, of course, the prime numbers in the standard natural numbers, right? So we just take all of those. So first of all, is this theory t satisfiable? So is there a structure which is going to be a model of all these formulas? So the natural numbers themselves, they satisfy all these parts, right? Because it's exactly all the.

Things that are true of the natural numbers. So this is true in the structure of the natural numbers. ## 23. Part p23 - An application of compactness

All right, let's continue with Compagnons. So we said this review, Compagnons says that if you have a set of sentences and every finite subset of it is satisfiable, so it has a model, then everything together has a model, right? Of course, this is an if AND only if, because if a big set is satisfiable, then all the subsets are also satisfiable by essentially the same model. So actually the model that satisfies the whole thing satisfies all the finite subsets, but the hypothesis that every finite subset is satisfiable might not use that one. So, it's a connected graph and it's satisfied there is no path less than n0. So, every finite subset is satisfiable. So, by compactness you should get that everything is satisfiable, but that will lead to a contradiction. So, there is no such a sentence gap. So, there is no first-order way, even with infinitely many sentences, but there are no infinitely many first-order sentences that can axiomatize connectedness. Okay, we'll see more examples in the next videos.

## 24. Part p24 - Proving non-definability via compactness

## 25. Part p25 - Compactness via implication

Welcome everybody. **Okay so now before we go into the next application we're going to look at a particular corollary of compactness that many of the applications are going to come in the form of these particular results and it's essentially something that is EQUIVALENT to compactness.** Actually when we prove compactness next month we're going to prove these results first and compactness is going to follow immediately from these results. So the results is the following. Suppose you have a set of sentences gamma and imagine there is an infinite set of sentences and that all together imply phi. ## 27. Part p27 - An application of Compactness for Sentential Logic

Welcome back everybody! So now we want to see an application on a combinatorics result of compactness for sentential logic. So we said compactness for sentential logic is kind of the simpler version which we actually proved in the last video but it's still quite useful in some situations if you find your way around. So sometimes like the out the compactness for the other one for first-order logic is more clear sometimes how to apply well usually it also requires tricks but this one you need to understand what you're doing and if you do it the right way you can get the corollaries from these results. **Okay so let's start with the definition of a partial order.** So a partial ordering consists of a set of elements a domain a universe we call it V in this case and a binary.

Relation where this binary relation satisfies three properties transitivity reflexivity and antisymmetry okay so these three properties that it needs to satisfy what are these three properties transitivity is the most important one and says that when you have that X is below Y and Y below Z then X is below Z okay you will expect that if you can order in among things right the next one reflexivity it's a very simple one and it just says that every element is below less than or equal itself just because well it's equal itself and the last one antisymmetry says that for every X and Y if X is less than or equal to Y and Y is less than or equal to X then X must be equal to Y okay so you cannot have things that are.

Not equal being less than or equal and greater than equal each other right so okay so those three things make a partial order so for instance for instance consider the set of letters A, B, C, D all the rest of the alphabet okay that's just A and now consider this set this is our universe now we want now is the set of finite strings over those letters okay so we saw that at the beginning so this is less than or equal to omega means the set of all finite tuples taken from this set A so in this case a finite tuple is nothing more than just a a sequence of string of letters right so A, B, Z, N, A, D that's a sequence of six letters so that's a.

Tuple of letters so that's something that belongs to A less than or equal omega so any string of letters would be done to that and let's consider the following ordering given two strings sigma and tau so these are string of characters sigma tau let's say that sigma is an initial segment of tau let's say that sigma is strictly less than tau even only if sigma is an initial segment of tau so the word contains the other one so for instance the word A, B, Z is an initial segment of the word A, B, Z, N, A, D right so that's why we say that it's less than right okay but if they could be words for instance that don't contain each other and they're incomparable under this ordering some words are comparable like if they extend each other some words are not and then you get here an order that is.

Transitive, reflexive, and asymmetric so that is a partial ordering okay good so a linear ordering is a partial ordering that also satisfies that whenever you have two elements they must be comparable okay so in the example before we had there could be words that are incomparable to each other but now like whenever we have two elements x and y they have to be comparable so they look like a line that's why they are called linear ordering right so the ordering so all the elements are comparable to each other so it looks like a line so the examples that we know we there are a few ones that we have used so far the real with less than or equal the integers with less than or equals we saw the double chain of the integers plus the integers.

So those are all linear orderings any two elements are comparable if we take for instance the lexicographic ordering on the set of words the set of strings of letters this is the lexicographic ordering is the one using the dictionary and that one is a linear order because even if one word doesn't contain the other we decide how to order them right so the dictionary kind of is a linear ordering of all the strings of characters or a and that is a linear order order so any less than or that may happen okay so in that case any two things are comparable now let's consider the following notion the notion of linearization okay so the linearization of a partial ordering is a way of squish it to make it into a linear order okay so the formally what we are doing is considering another ordering triangle left.

Which is compatible with this one, and so that it's linear, okay, on the same domain. So we have, by compatible, formally what we mean is that for every two elements p and q in p, if p is less than or equal q in the partial order in p, then p is triangle leg less than or equal q. Alright, so it preserves the ordering, but there are going to be things that are incomparable, so where this is not the case, but still this is the case. Okay, so there are many more things comparable here because we wanted to make it a linear thing. So let me draw an example. Here is a partial ordering, so it has six elements, and I draw it so that the elements that are higher up or higher are greater than or equal the ones.

That are below, right? So in here we have p1 is about p0, p2 is about p0, but p1 and p2 are incomparable in this here. And then p3 and p4 are also incomparable, but they are both above p1 and p2, p5 is on top of them. Each formula only uses finitely many variables, so we only have to look finitely far in this construction of the Cantor sets, and some of the intervals are going to correspond to true, some to false, so we can always find these open intervals that cover the parts that are false. Okay, and that's how we get the observation that for each sentential formula phi, we have this finite, finitely many intervals that cover the false part. So how are we going to use.
