## 33. Part p33 - Application of Compactness: Fields of Characteristic 0 Not Strongly Elementary

Okay, so one more of these videos where I'll show you how I read math. Now we're on the same page as before, page 157, section 6.3 in Mileti's book, 157 right there, which you can look it up. And now we want to do these things. **So it says, we can now use Compagnon's Theorem to show that certain elementary classes are NOT strong elementary classes.** Again, you look it up, strong elementary classes means that they are a set of models of some finite set of sentences, or equivalently, a set of models of a single sentence, because you can put ands between them and get a single sentence.

So this is saying a certain, something that we know is elementary, so it's a class of models of some set of sentences, but not of a finite set of sentences. The key result behind such arguments is the following preposition, which says that if you have a strong elementary class, K, that we already know is equal to the class of models of some sigma, then for some infinite set sigma, then we can find a finite subset of sigma itself witnessing the fact that K is a strong elementary class.

So it's saying what the preposition says, so if you read the preposition, it should say the same thing. Suppose that K is a strong elementary class, sigma is a set of sentences in some vocabulary L, and K is the class of models of sigma, that's what we are saying. then there exists a finite sigma zero, including sigma such that K is a model of sigma zero. Okay, so here, why is it not, like what it's saying, why does it say that, what we said before?

**Here it says that IF we know that, here, that the K is a strongly elementary class, which means we know that it's the class of models of some finite set of sentences, and THEN here, well, the conclusion is that it is a class of models of a finite set of sentences, sigma zero.** So when you see this, be like, stop, stop, and be like, okay, wait, what does it say?

Well, what we know is that it's the class of models of some finite set of sentences, but we don't know that these finite set of sentences need to be a subset of sigma. Okay, so that's the new part about this. So we know it's a model of a finite set of sigma, but we didn't know sentences, but we didn't know that we can always choose that finite set to be a subset of sigma if we know that K is the class of models of this big sigma.

So there could be many finite set of sentences that axiomatize K, but what it's saying is that if there is one, if there is such a finite set, there might be one that is a finite set of this sigma. Okay? **And that's going to be useful because that means that IF we have sigma for K, THEN, and we know that it's, and we want to prove that it's NOT strongly elementary, so that means IF we assume that it's strongly elementary, it has to be axiomatized by a finite subset of those, and NOT by maybe some other formula that we CANNOT control, as we're going to see in the example afterwards.**

Okay, so let's read this again, so we want to be saying that sigma logically implies tau, and what does that mean? Okay, so if you don't remember your abstract algebra, you can look it up in Wikipedia, let's say. What's a field? And essentially a field is a ring where every element has an inverse, except for zero. And what is the mean having characteristic zero? Characteristic of a field is given as follows, if you've taken the element one, you add it with itself, one plus one plus one plus one plus one plus one plus one.

If eventually you get equal zero, you see how many times you add it, if you get p, that means characteristic p. if you add one plus one plus one plus one plus one, and you never get zero, then you say that it has characteristic zero. So also, if you don't remember that, at least look it up. So the rationals, the reals, they are fields of characteristic zero.

One plus one plus one is never zero, but if you look at the integers mod p, so zp, one plus one plus one plus one plus one, p times you get p, which is equal to zero, right? So that has characteristic p. It's always important to look at examples when you see new definitions. Okay, well, vocabulary of rings, this is what you expect. ## 36. Part p36 - Tautologies in First-Order Logic

Okay, so the next thing we need to review is tautologies. **The group axiom number one right here says we can add all tautologies as axioms.** So what do we mean by that? So let's recall that in sentential logic that we saw at the beginning of the course, a tautology is a well-formed formula in sentential logic, where you have no variables, only propositional sentences, variables, that is true for every truth assignment. It doesn't matter what truth assignment you put, you always get true. So that means when you do the whole truth table with all the possible combinations of true or false, if you look at the result you get true, true, true, true, true the whole way. So all true. So that's a tautology in propositional logic. So what do we mean by a tautology here in first-order logic? In.

First-order logic, a tautology is obtained from a sentential tautology, one like the one we did at the beginning of the course, by replacing each sentential variable by a first-order well-formed formula. Okay, so for instance, suppose you have let's say a simple one, a or not a, that's a tautology. So we can replace, I don't know, a by for every x p of x implies q of x. So that means then we need to replace this, then we gonna have, or not, that's gonna stay the same. So we keep the same connectives, or not, and then we write the negation of this, or not, for every x p of x implies q of x. Okay, and the not applies to this.

Right, so we replace the place where a shows up. We could have something a bit more interesting, for instance, we could have a tautology of the form a or not b, if AND only if not b and not a. Okay, so that's a tautology, you have to check that everything works out, but sure, that's a tautology. And then we can replace, I don't know, let's say we replace a by for every x p of x, unless we replace b by that exists the y q of y comma x, I don't know, something, something first order. And then we have to replace this one again by the same, exists x q y x, and this one by the same, for every x p of x. And the other one we just.

Have to replace this one again by the top, not, if AND only if, negation of this, and negation of this. Right, so that's what we mean by a tautology in first-order logic. We take one from sentential logic, and we replace this propositional variables, or sentential variables, by formulas from first-order logic, in the corresponding places, in the right places. **So anything that is of this form, we can use for axiom, for the group of axioms one.** And actually, we can take generalizations of these things.

## 40. Part p40 - The Soundness Theorem

Welcome back everybody. So things are going to get more interesting now because now we're going to try to connect a logical implication and formal implication. Okay so remember logical implication is the one that says that gamma logically implies phi if every model of gamma is a model of phi. **Sometimes it's called semantical implication because this actually uses the semantics, the actual value, the meaning of the formulas in gamma and phi.** So the meaning how you interpret them in structure. So if a structure models all of this then the structure models this.

While the logical, well the formal implication, syntactic implication is purely syntactic. It's purely a manipulation of symbols without associating any meaning to the symbols. It's just manipulation of symbols you prove. Well you can get a deduction of phi from gamma. **So this theorem is something that you should expect to be TRUE, right?** I mean if this provability is gonna be useful at all this should be true, right? So if you can prove something then that something must be true, right? Otherwise what's the point? So this is definitely something you would expect.

It's the soundness theorem. **I kind of I usually think of it as saying that the rule and the logical axiom SOUND right.** That's essentially what you need to do, that they just SOUND correct. So that's how we prove this. Okay so how do you do it? Well again we use kind of the induction principle that we usually use. We consider the set of all sentences which are semantically implied by phi, or logically implied by gamma, sorry. And we want to show that this includes all the theorems, that every phi that satisfies this belongs to the set. And so for that we need to show a few things. So s s is closed under modus ponens. Why is that? Well if you have a formula alpha that belongs to s and a formula.

Alpha implies beta belongs to s, so that means we know that gamma implies alpha semantically and gamma implies alpha implies beta. then every model m of gamma satisfies alpha and alpha implies beta. **And by definition of what it means to satisfy imply, THEN you get that m satisfies beta. right?** So beta belongs to s. Because this was done for every model m that satisfies gamma. So gamma syntactically implies beta, which means that it belongs to s.

S contains all of gamma. Yes, if phi belongs to gamma, then gamma implies, semantically implies phi, right? Every model of gamma is going to imply, it's going to satisfy phi. That's obviously true. And s contains all of the logical axioms lambda. **So that one we need to check a little bit, but we have to show that every logical axiom belongs to s.** So essentially what we want to show is here the logical axioms, right there. We want to show that if phi is a logical axiom, then gamma models phi, right? That's what we need to show to show that they all belong to s. But actually we're going to show more than that.

**We're going to show that IF you have a logical axiom, you're actually holding every structure and m is a structure, THEN m models phi.** So the logical axioms are true in every structure, right? So that's what they are implied by any gamma. So why is that? Well, you have to go through one by one and see why it's true, but they are all kind of obviously true.

So the tautologies, yes, they should be true in every structure. **And this, again, you have to look at why the definition of the model symbol implies this, but yes, so all the tautologies should be TRUE in every structure.** This is also going to be true. if something is true for every x, it should be true whenever it's at t x by t. And essentially all of them. So you have to go one by one checking that they're all true in every structure.

In this case, your intuition is okay. I mean, it's good to actually do the proof formally to understand what's going on. **But in this case, it's fine to use intuition because the definition of being a model of phi being a model of m is essentially using your, well, it's based on your intuition of what the symbols mean.** Because now there the symbols have meaning. The and has the meaning and, the or has the meaning or, the implied has the meaning and so on and so forth. So it makes sense to use the meaning of the symbols to understand why this is true. And if you use the meanings of the symbols, then you'll see that all these guys are true. You can check them one by one.

So this is not a Syntactic proof. This is more a semantical proof about this structure. So yes, you can check all the axioms are true in every structure or the logical axioms are true in every structure. That's what makes them the logical axioms. **So that's how you get by induction principle that IF gamma PROVES phi, THEN phi belongs to s, which means that gamma semantically PROVES phi.**

So essentially what's going on here is that when you prove something, everything that you prove is true essentially. And this is just because every step of a proof is obviously true. I am a model exponent should be obviously true. Well, your assumptions are true if you're assuming them and the logical axioms are obviously true. So that's what all there is to soundness. Okay, so proving something means that is true. **A corollary, this is just more than a corollary, this is just a restatement again.** Sometimes this version is called soundness too. It says that if a set is satisfiable, then it's consistent. Let's recall what these things mean. So let's call this set, let's call it gamma. So this means there is a model which satisfies gamma. So that's satisfiable means.

You have a model. There is at least one model. And consistent means that for every formula beta, either gamma does not prove beta, or gamma does not prove not beta, right? Remember inconsistent meant that for some beta you prove both beta and the negation of beta. **So CONSISTENT means that for NO beta you do that.** So the proof is this corollary is by contradiction. Suppose that gamma is inconsistent.

The proof of this is very direct. So suppose the gamma is satisfiable and it has a model m, right? then for every formula sentence beta, either m models beta or m models not beta, but not both, right? So in other words, we can say that either m does not model not beta or m does not model beta. then since m is a model of gamma, we get that either gamma doesn't imply semantically not beta or gamma doesn't imply semantically beta, right? Because if m is a model of gamma, right? Because if m is a model of gamma, but it's not a model of non-beta, then gamma cannot imply not beta. The same with beta. **And now by the soundness theorem, we get that either gamma does NOT prove NOT beta or gamma does NOT prove beta, right?** So here we're using.

Essentially the contrapositive of this. if you don't do this, then you don't do that. Contrapositive of soundness. And that's our proof because then we chose any beta and we show that you cannot prove both it and the negation. So then gamma is consistent. So soundness is essentially the same as saying that if you're satisfiable, if you have a model, then you must be consistent. So you cannot prove something and it's negation. So that makes sense.

We'll continue with more consistent set of formulas in the next video. See you guys next.

## 41. Part p41 - Maximal Consistent Sets of Sentences

Welcome back everybody. So let's consider now a type of set of sentences that will be very useful in the upcoming proofs of COMPLETENESS. So these are the maximal consistent set of sentences. It's essentially what you expect. Recall the definition of inconsistent. A set of sentences is inconsistent. if from it you can prove some sentence and also its negation. So that's bad. Right? So that's inconsistent. There are of course some set of sentences that are inconsistent.

If you, let's say, put all the sentences. if you put beta and negative beta inside gamma, then they prove beta and negative beta. That's inconsistent. So yeah, we usually don't like those. And a set of sentences is said to be MAXIMAL if essentially you cannot make it bigger. And the way we write that is we're saying for every formula phi, either phi, a set of sentences is MAXIMAL if you cannot make it bigger. And by that I mean there is that for every sentence phi, either it or its negation belongs to gamma. So one of the two has to belong to gamma.

And if you have a set that is consistent and MAXIMAL, then you have that exactly one of those two is in gamma. You're gonna have both because if you have both you will be inconsistent. So if you're consistent and MAXIMAL, you must have one of those two but not both. So you cannot be bigger than that. Okay? So what is an example of maximal consistent set of formulas? Well, whenever you have a structure M, if you consider the theory of M, that is MAXIMAL and consistent. So why is that? Well, remember the theory of M is the set of all sentences that are true in this structure M, right? So for different structures, you're gonna have different theories. This is all the sentences, the set of all the sentences.

That are true in this particular structure. So we know that whenever you have such a set for every formula, either it or its negation is true in M, right? And you're gonna have both to be true in M. if you have one, then you can have the other. So exactly for each formula phi, either phi or not phi is true in M. So that's why you get that the set of all sentences that are true in M is a MAXIMAL consistent, consistent set of sentences. **One observation, IF you have a set of sentences, so here sigma is a set of sentences, that is satisfiable, recall that satisfiable means that it has a model, THEN there is an extension, you can make it bigger, you can add some sentence to it,.**

So that you end up with a gamma that is MAXIMAL and consistent. Why is that? Well, if sigma is satisfiable, then it has some model M, right? And then if you take the theory of M, that's our gamma, right? The theory of M, since M satisfies sigma, M is going to be contained in the theory of M, because M is going to satisfy every formula of sigma, that's how we chose M. And therefore, the theory of M is going to be an extension of sigma, which is MAXIMAL and consistent. Okay, so if you're satisfiable, you can always be extended to something that is MAXIMAL and consistent. Know that if you have a set of sentences sigma, let's say the exact same of groups, satisfiable, then you have many,.

Many models satisfying sigma, and they are all different, and they all have different theories, right? So if you are sigma that is not complete already, then you can extend it in many different ways, so that you're MAXIMAL and complete. Because you can take, let's say, if sigma was the exact same of groups, for each group you have a different theory, well, somehow the same, but you have many, many different theories of many different groups, those are all extensions of sigma that are MAXIMAL and complete.

What is going to be useful to us to show is that if sigma is consistent, then there is an extension of gamma, which is MAXIMAL and complete. We know that satisfiable implies consistent, but we don't know the other direction yet. We're going to know this soon. So we're going to have to prove this one by hand. if sigma is consistent, then there is an extension that is MAXIMAL and consistent. How do we show that? So here is the dilemma again, so that we can write a proof. **And again, so the claim that we need to show that IF sigma is CONSISTENT, THEN and phi is a sentence, THEN one of sigma union phi or sigma union NOT phi is also CONSISTENT.** So this is the dilemma that we're claiming here. if I have, if you start with.

Something that is consistent and you have a sentence phi, if you add to sigma one of either phi or not phi, one of those two, you end up with something consistent. It's likely that you can add both and you're still consistent. For instance, if you have the maximum groups and you add the sentence that says commutative, you get the commutative groups that's consistent, satisfiable. And if you add non-commutative as a sentence, you get the non-commutative groups that's also satisfied. So sometimes you get both are consistent or satisfiable. But what we're saying here is that at least one of the two must be consistent.

You can always become a bit bigger and prove that it's consistent. So why is this true? if sigma phi is consistent, then we are done. **On the other hand, IF sigma phi is inconsistent, we show by this reduction to absurdum theorem right here, that IF gamma phi is inconsistent, THEN gamma PROVES the negation of phi.** And this implies that sigma PROVES not phi. So we claim that this implies that sigma together with not phi is consistent. And why is that? Otherwise, if sigma together with not phi PROVES beta and sigma together with not phi PROVES not beta, then we get that sigma PROVES not phi implies beta. And also that sigma PROVES not phi implies not beta.

But putting this together with that, by modus ponens, we get that sigma PROVES not beta. So then these two together imply that sigma is inconsistent, which contradicts our assumption, right? So this assuming sigma is consistent. So this one is not right. And that's because of the other ways that appear here. So we get that, yes, phi and not phi is consistent. Okay, so what we show here that if sigma together with phi was inconsistent, then sigma PROVES the negation of phi and that implies that sigma together with the negation of phi is consistent. And so because if you prove the negation, essentially you can add it to sigma and you don't add anything because you're adding something that is already provable from sigma. So if you were consistent before by yourself.

And you add something that you prove, you should stay consistent. That's what this proof is saying. Okay, so that PROVES the claim that if you're consistent and you take one more formula, one more sentence, then you can add one of these two to make it consistent. Now, what is that we want to show? We want to show that if you're consistent, then there is an extension of you that is MAXIMAL and consistent. So how do you prove this? How do you finish the proof? **So here, so to be MAXIMAL means that for every sentence fee, one of the two MUST be in gamma.** So what are we going to do?

We're going to start with sigma and we're going to go through all the sentences one by one. We're going to put them in order one by one and we go through each of them and we add either the formula or the negation into the set so that we stay consistent. And this is assuming we have a countable vocabulary. So let fee 0, fee 1, fee 2, fee 3 be an enumeration of all sentences. Okay, this requires a little observation because we need to be able to enumerate them. And the point here is that if the vocabulary is countable, you have countably many symbols in your vocabulary plus you have countably many logical symbols because you have the variables that are infinitely many variables you're allowed.

To use. So it's infinite but it's still countable. So the total number of symbols that you have is countable. How many finite words you can write with countably many symbols is only countably many words. You have to kind of figure out how to do it but you can enumerate them all in some way. And from all the words you can write, meaning strings of symbols, some of them are actual sentences, so you have to restrict yourself to the actual sentences. But you can easily find a way of listing them all, kind of like a dictionary kind of ordering of listing all the well-formed sentences in a list so that you don't skip any and they all show up somewhere in the list. So once you do that,.

Now you start increasing your sigma to make it maximum and you do it like this. So let sigma zero be sigma and then you do it by induction. You say if sigma zero together with phi zero is consistent, So notice that by the way that sigma zero is consistent. We let sigma one be sigma zero union phi zero. And otherwise we know that otherwise since the sigma zero is consistent, we know that sigma one not phi zero is consistent.

So we let sigma one be sigma zero union the negation of phi zero. Okay, so we do one of the two and we always, whatever we define, we are staying consistent. **Right, and THEN we define like this sigma one, and THEN we take the next formula, and we define sigma two, and the next formula is sigma three.** And we go all the way up to sigma n essentially.

**Suppose we have defined already sigma n, and THEN we know that IF sigma n, we see that IF sigma n and phi n are CONSISTENT, we define sigma n plus one to be sigma n together with the formula phi n.** And if sigma n together with phi, no the negation of phi n is consistent, we let sigma n plus one be sigma n together with the negation of sigma n.

So at each step, we add to the set either phi n or the negation of phi n, whichever of the two makes it consistent. **And we know by the claim on the previous slide that IF you're CONSISTENT, you can always add one of the negation and stay CONSISTENT.** So the whole process, you stay consistent. And then at the end, we let gamma be the final result, right?

The union over all n in the natural numbers of sigma n. It's going to be MAXIMAL because every formula was added along the way. So for every formula, we added either e to the negation inside it, so it's MAXIMAL. And why is it consistent? Well, because if gamma PROVES beta and it PROVES not beta for some formula beta, then in the proof, in this particular proof, we only use finitely many formulas, right?

Because this is a deduction, a formal deduction. A formal deduction can only use finitely many formulas. **So that means that these finitely many formulas MUST belong to one of these sigma ns.** These sigma ns are all nesting and getting bigger and bigger. So that will imply that there exists an n such that sigma n PROVES beta and not beta, right? So some sigma n is going to contain the finitely many formulas that show up in that deduction.

But this is not the case. This contradicts. Sigma n is consistent. Okay, so every set can be, every consistent set of sentences can be extended to one that is MAXIMAL and consistent. So that finishes the proof of this lemma. See you in the next video.

## 42. Part p42 - Gödel's Completeness Theorem

Welcome back everybody. **So our last theorem, which we won't prove today, actually we can spend the whole next two weeks proving it, is the COMPLETENESS theorem.** Gödel's famous COMPLETENESS theorem. The COMPLETENESS theorem says that if a formula or a sentence phi is implied logically from gamma, then it can be proved from gamma. All right? So what this is saying is if it's true that in every model of gamma satisfies phi, then there is an actual proof, a Syntactic proof of phi from gamma, which is the most you can hope for, right? Because we know already from soundness that if you prove something, then that has to be true in terms of models. So now you're proving the reverse, that if it's true in terms of.

Model, then you can prove it. So that means everything that is possible to prove, you can prove just with this modus ponens rule and the logical axioms. **So in that sense is that this is the COMPLETENESS theorem.** It's telling you that the rules for writing formal proofs, namely modus ponens and the logical axioms, are complete. There is nothing else that you need to do for your proofs. So whenever you do a proof, that's like if you want to prove something, if that something is true in this sense, then there is a proof from there using those logical axioms and the rules. So it's gonna be hard to prove this one, but let's talk about it a little bit more. So the second part is an equivalent statement. So they both mean the.

Same thing. And that if a set of formulas is consistent, then it is satisfied. We already show from soundness that if you're satisfiable, you must be consistent. So this is kind of the reversal of that. And the proof is essentially the same as we had before. The proof of this is that if gamma is not satisfiable, then for every formula beta, gamma semantically implies beta. The reason for that is that if you're not satisfied, we don't have any model. So it's trivially true that every model of gamma is a model of beta. Every model of gamma is a model of beta just because there are no models of gamma. So this is true for every formula. **It will be implied and also THEN that means that.**

Also you will get also the negation that will also be implied. **But THEN by the COMPLETENESS theorem, by part one, you will get that gamma PROVES beta and gamma PROVES NOT beta.** So that's how you get that one implies two. Which you haven't provided. What's the reason that two implies one? And this is by a contrapositive. if gamma does not prove phi, then by the reduction to absurdum that we proved last week, if you don't prove something, then gamma and the negation are consistent. Okay, so we're going to use this one in the opposite direction. if you don't prove, then this is not inconsistent. So if you don't prove phi, then gamma together with not phi is consistent.

But then by part two, gamma together with not phi is satisfiable. But then that implies that gamma does not logically imply phi. **Because gamma logically implies phi means that every model of gamma is a model of phi and this being satisfiable says that there exists a model of gamma, which is NOT a model of phi.** So that means gamma cannot imply phi. And that's what we get from this contrapositive. Cool. So those two are equivalent.

**And together they are called COMPLETENESS theorem.** We're actually going to prove this one. We're going to show that if you're consistent, then you're satisfiable and that's going to imply the other one. Okay, and then this is actually going to get this together with soundness. We get if AND only if. Essentially we get that logical implication is equivalent to Syntactic implication and we get that consistency is equivalent to satisfiability using soundness for the other direction.

Okay, so one corollary of this that we've been using for a long time is compactness. **So the compactness theorem follows directly from here.** The compactness theorem has two versions. I also like this one too, which we saw already that they are equivalent. The first one says that if gamma logically implies phi, then there's a finite subset of gamma that already logically implies phi. And equivalently, we said that this is the same thing as saying that if every finite subset of a gamma is satisfiable, then the whole gamma is satisfiable. Right? So we use both versions.

Both are equivalent. We even prove that they are equivalent. So let's combine this. And why does it follow from COMPLETENESS? Well, it's very simple because by COMPLETENESS and soundness together, we know that logical implication is the same as a formal syntactic implication. Right? And so they are back and forth, they are the same. So if a whole set of sentences PROVES this sentence phi, that means we have a deduction, a formal deduction of phi from gamma. In the formal deduction, we can only use finitely many sentences. Right?

A formal deduction is a finite list of formulas. And some of those are used, are counted as assumptions, but only finitely many. So we are using finitely many of the assumptions in the proof. not more than that. So we can take a finite subset, which are the ones that are used in the proof. And that's enough to prove phi. Which again, if you prove phi, then by soundness, you logically imply phi. So that's the proof of compactness.

So compactness is very easy. if you look at formal deductions and COMPLETENESS and soundness tell you the formal deductions are the same as logical deductions. Okay, so we'll actually do this proof in the next coming ways. See you guys later.

## 45. Part p45 - Term Models for Maximal Consistent Sets of Formulas

Welcome everybody! So we have now built, given a set of formulas, a model built out of terms. Okay so we have this model with a lot of terms and an equivalence relation on the terms that tells you which term should be equal and we also know how to apply the functions of the terms and apply relations on the terms so we have a full tau model if tau is the vocabulary. Now the question is what does this model satisfy? **So we saw in examples at the very beginning, let's see for instance here that on this vocabulary with identity and operation and a constant symbol, IF we take a group axioms we get something that is called a monoid.** It's close to being a group and it satisfies associativity, it satisfies that.

The identity is an entity, but in this case we have no inverses. All the elements we have looks like identity C, C squared, C cubed, C fourth, essentially look like the natural numbers and actually if you do the operation on this one you can see that it looks like the natural number of the addition. So it's not really a group because it has no inverse. So it's not that good. then we saw another example where we added the inverse function as a function symbol. So now we can build terms that involve the inverse function and now in this example, this particular example, we have two letters a and b, we could have one. So now we can build anything that you can write with the two letters, the two constants a and b, a times b,.

A minus one, b, and multiplying and using the b minus, the minus one operation, the inverse operation. And we have, we saw that what we get is everything that can be written as a word concatenating a's, b's, a inverses and b inverses in a way that if you have two of these together they cancel out. So we got a group. In this case we got a group and we are happy because we started with the group axioms and we ended up with the group. So one thing to notice, in the previous case we also had the inverse axioms. The group axioms contain a sentence that says that for every x that exists a y, such as x star y equals the identity. So that's part of the group axioms.

Here, except the problem, the reason we didn't get an inverse in our model is that we don't have a term that builds the inverse, right? We are building this term model, so all that we have here are terms, closed terms. So if you want an inverse, you have to have an inverse, you have to have some way of building it as a term. **So this axiom just tells you that there exists something that is the inverse, but it doesn't give it to you as a term.** And that's why the inverse is missing in this term model up here. And even though the axiom is there, while in this case the inverse was there. Okay, so that's all to say that it's not that simple. The term model.

Doesn't satisfy everything that we want. But let's start seeing what it satisfies. So one thing that is going to help to satisfy more things is to assume that our set of formulas is MAXIMAL consistent. Okay, so remember MAXIMAL meant that for every sentence phi, either gamma PROVES c or gamma PROVES the negation of phi. So I must prove one of those two. So that's not true for the case of groups, for instance, right? So you can write a sentence that says the group is a billion, and the group axioms don't prove that the group is either a billion or not a billion. They don't prove either. So the axiom for group is not MAXIMAL. But if you assume it's MAXIMAL, we're going to have better.

Properties. **So the theorem says that IF you're MAXIMAL and CONSISTENT, and phi is a quantifier-free sentence.** So it's a sentence that uses no quantifier. So it's built out of atomic formulas using just, negation, and or implies. And the sententia connect is essentially, but not quantifiers. And it's a sentence, so it also has no free variables. then this formula is satisfied in the term model if AND only if is true in gamma.

So let's see why that's the case. Well, we have to start with atomic formulas. For the atomic formulas, we pretty much know this is true, right? **We know that IF gamma PROVES that some term s is equal to another term t, THEN by definition s is e related to t, right?** And then also by definition of the term model, we get that in the term model m gamma s equals t, right? Okay, so that's for that direction.

What about the other direction? **Well, the other direction is almost the same because actually we know that the definition of e was an IF AND ONLY IF definition, right?** So that's exactly what defines the e. And we also know that the definition of the term model, two things are equal in the model if AND only if they're equivalent. So these are all like if and only ifs. So it is very clear in that case. For the relations, we have the same, right? In the model. **And that is exactly the same thing as saying that by definition of what it means, we modeled something that m gamma satisfies r of t1, tk, right?** So for atomic formulas, this is the case, this is true. if gamma is of the form of psi and theta, then we can apply the induction because we assume we know the rest of the theorem is true for theta and psi, and use that for phi. So let's see what we get.

Well, then gamma PROVES psi and theta, if AND only if gamma PROVES psi and gamma PROVES theta. Why is that? Well, this is because of the rule T. ## 46. Part p46 - Theories That Contain Term Witnesses

Welcome back everybody! So this is our last week of classes and our goal for this week is to finish the proof COMPLETENESS and with that we finish compactness, we finish everything, everything kind of comes to a close. **So let me remind you COMPLETENESS is the theorem that said that IF a set of sentences is gamma as logically implies a sentence phi in the sense that every model of gamma is a model of phi, THEN there is a formal monetary deduction of phi from gamma.** And we showed us equivalent to saying that any consistent set of formulas is satisfiable and we are trying to prove COMPLETENESS via that way. So consistent meant there is no deduction of a contradiction, but satisfiable means there exists a model. So they are very different in form, so we're using the.

Fact that we have no deduction of a contradiction to build a model. And we've been going quite far so far. We've built our term model by taking all the closed terms that you can write in the language, then consider this equivalent relation of two terms that are equal if you can prove they are equal, and then define the model of the function symbols and the relation symbols on this guide. And we proved that if you have a maximum set of sentences, then the term model satisfies all formulas phi so long if AND only if they are proved, but only for the case when phi is quantifier free. So what we need to do now is fix the case with the quantifiers. And the example of the group that we saw earlier, that's a good example where things fail.

When you add quantifiers. **We had this example where we have the group axiom but we didn't have a term for the inverse.** And even though we had the formula that says for every x to exist a y, x star y is the identity, that formula does not hold in the term model mostly because of these quantifiers, mostly because of these there exists. Because just saying that there exists something in the set of sentences doesn't imply that it's going to be a term for that. And therefore it might not exist, that y might not be given by a term and therefore it wouldn't be in the turmoil. So in the term model you wouldn't have such a y. So how do we fix that? So this is the concept that we add today, which is the fact containing term witnesses. So.

We say that a set of sentences gamma contains terms, term witnesses. if whenever gamma PROVES a formula of the form that exists an x phi, where imagine that x is a free variable inside phi, right? So x kind of shows up inside phi. then there is some closed term t such that gamma PROVES the formula phi where x is being substituted for t. So you put a t in place of every x inside the formula phi. So that's what we need.

So let's see an example of how this would look like for instance. In the case when we had tau just the entity star and c and gamma the group axioms. then let's just put an example. So in this case gamma shows that there exists a y such as y star c equals the identity. It shows this, right? Because one of the group axioms is for every x star y such as x times y equals identity. So you can show, you can prove, you can give a deduction proof for this. But there is no term in this, our term model that satisfies what this y satisfies. Right? We saw that before. No closed term t in this vocabulary such that gamma shows t star c equals e. if you remember, when we saw this example last week, we saw that.

All the terms that we get built out of e's and c's are essentially e alone and then all the powers of c, c, c star c, c star c, c three times because e is an entity and c is the only constant you have. All you can do is multiply by itself and by associativity those are all the only ones you get and by identity the e's cancel out. So those are all the terms you get and you don't get c inverse as a term. So in this case we see that there is no such closed terms and therefore this tells you that the gamma doesn't contain term witnesses.

Good. Let's see another example. Let's look at the other example that we saw last time which is the group, the group still the group axioms but now with the inverse in the language. Right? So in this case we got tau has the identity star but it also has an unary operation for inverse and then two letters a and b and gamma is the group axioms. But now the group axioms are built using this inverse operation. Right? **So the inverse axiom says for every x, x times x inverse equals identity.** So it's a slight modification of the group axioms to describe how this inverse behaves.

So now in this case we do we have again the gamma shows there exists a y y star c equals e. Fine. Shows the same thing. Still like an axiom. And the closed term no sorry it's not a c. It should be an a because we don't have a let's say. And there exists a term the term a inverse. Right? And if you replace y for a inverse we get that gamma PROVES a inverse star a equals e.

Right? So we are replacing the y by a inverse and it works. Right? So we have a weakness for that one. So that doesn't mean that it contains term weaknesses for all existential terms of this form. But this is an example of one where it exists. Gamma proves something of this form. Right? This one has this form. Exists y such as something. And then in that something you can replace the y by a term and gamma PROVES that. So gamma has a term. Essentially the name of this counts because if uh gamma PROVES so it could be that you prove there exists x such as something but you don't know how to name that thing. **Well containing term witnesses it means that there exists a term in the language in.**

The vocab I can write with the symbols of the vocabulary which witnesses this term t witnesses that there exists an x such as phi. Because t is one such guy that satisfies phi. **Okay so let's see what we can do with this definition of containing term witnesses.** And here's the key. ## 48. Part p48 - Summing Up

Welcome back everybody! So we've finished with COMPLETENESS, we've finished everything, so we have now a deduction system to produce formal proofs, like very syntactic, just based on symbols, manipulation of symbols, formal proofs, that is complete, so if something can be proved or should be proved, then it can be proved. So if something is true in terms of models and satisfiability, then it can be proved. So the logical axioms that we set, that set Lambda that we have for the set of logical axioms is complete, there is nothing else that we need, modus ponens and the logical axioms give us everything.

As you tried, as you saw, it's very tedious to do a proof in that system, but that's enough. I mean, yeah, we can prove meta theorems and stuff to make it less tedious to prove things in there, but that's enough. So if you write a proof, you should be able to write in that, even though it might drive you crazy. There are computers now that allow you to help you write formal proofs, they help you with all those meta theorems and transform one proof into another, and then you might end up with a long formal proof, and the computers can just go and check one by one that every line is correct, and confirm that the proof is fully correct.

**And THEN, came Gödel, in the 1930s, after he showed COMPLETENESS, like, a few years after, maybe five years after, he showed the INCOMPLETENESS theorem, but now he was referring to the axioms, in the case when you're trying to develop all of mathematics.** And he proved that if gamma is a consistent set of axioms, set of sentences, that contains enough of arithmetic, so, if the vocabulary could be anything, but at least it contains zero, one, plus, times, and it should just have the basic properties of arithmetic, about zero, plus, one, plus, times, just the basic ones, some minimal, to be able to work with it.

Then, and, also, and, it has this property that is computably enumerable, which is, I guess, it's important to have, because if you have an infinite list of axioms, you want to know that at least you can recognize them. **So, computable in that sense, that you can, at least, IF you see it, I don't know, you can put it in a computer, the computer can tell you, yes, it's an axiom, NO, it's NOT an axiom.**

**IF you don't have that, THEN it's pretty useful, IF you don't even know what's an axiom and what's NOT an axiom.** So, the assumption is that there has to be at least a computer that can enumerate, one by one, all the axioms in your list. So, if you have this, consistent contains enough arithmetic and it's computably enumerable, it cannot be MAXIMAL. So, it cannot contain everything and its negation.

And, actually, if a set of axioms is computably enumerable, then you look at all the theorems that it PROVES, they're still computably enumerable. So, like, this is saying that if your set of axioms is computably enumerable, it's always going to be a formula, a sentence, that neither it nor its negation are provable. So, that it's missing. You cannot prove either it or the negation, so you cannot decide which one is the true one.

There's always going to be something missing and, therefore, your set of axioms is incomplete. It's not proving everything. There are some sentences that you cannot decide whether they are true or false, if you just restrict yourself to provability.
